1.  SUMMARY 
 

This Technical Report updates previous versions to reflect the latest findings from the 

Phase II exploration program, which include primarily updated Section 14: Mineral Resource 

estimates for lithium and potassium, and Section 25: Interpretation and Conclusions, and 

Section 26: Recommendations. Supporting these finding are additional data regarding drilling 

and pumping tests (Section 10), sample preparation, analysis and security (Section 11), and 

data verification (Section 12).  

1.1 PROPERTY AND LOCATION 
 

The Property is in the central portion of the Salar de Pastos Grandes Basin, and is 

located in the Los Andes Department, in the Central portion of the Puna Block of Salta 

Province, northwestern Argentina (Figure 4.1), 13 kilometers (km) south–southeast of the 

Santa Rosa de los Pastos Grandes village, 56 km southwest of the regional administrative 

centre at San Antonio de los Cobres and 154 km west-northwest of the Salta provincial capital.  

The Property is 388 km straight line distant east-south-east of Antofagasta, Chile, the closest 

sea port.  The central coordinates of the Salar are 24°34'44" south latitude and 66°42'26" west 

longitude, at an average elevation of 3,785 meters above sea level (masl).  

1.2  OWNERSHIP 
 

The present technical report (the "Report") was requested by Millennial Lithium 

Corporation ("Millennial") to document work to date on nine mining leases. The first of these 

(Jorge Eduardo; Neptalí 2; El Milagro; Norte Argentino; Papadopoulos XXXII) were obtained 



2 

 

in 2016 by Millennial from a local Salta entity known as the Moreno Group through an option 

to purchase agreement ("Purchase Agreement").  

 

The previous owners of Papadopoulos XXXII property dropped their claim and it was 

declared vacant by the mining court. It was subsequently released for filing and, with multiple 

claimants was awarded to Millennial, as the winner of a drawing. An additional 4,652.95 

hectares (ha) (Taba PG; Papadopoulos LXXIV, and; Aguamarca 15) were added in October 

2017.  The Property package is centered in the Salar de Pastos Grandes basin and covers 

1,519.4 ha.  An additional 2,492.05 hectares were awarded in August 2017 in a tender held by 

the Province-owned resources and energy distribution company, Recursos Energeticos y 

Mineros Salta S.A.  (REMSA), with the final contract signed in December 2017.  Aerial extent 

of the current Properties held by Millennial as of the date of this report is 8,664.4 ha. The 

license group is collectively referred to in the report as the "Property" and the previous owners 

as the "Vendors". 

1.3  GEOLOGY AND MINERALIZATION 
 

The Pastos Grandes salar basin is in the Altiplano-Puna region of Argentina – Bolivia 

– Chile, known as the Lithium Triangle. Basins in this region host the largest portion of known 

lithium brine resources in the world.  These basins formed as inland closed lakes in a basin and 

range structural environment generated by alternating compressional and extensional regimes 

over the back arc of the Andean magmatic belts.  The Pastos Grandes Salar is the current 

expression of a larger sedimentary basin, known as the Sijes Basin, developed and deposited 

since the Miocene period (7 - 5 million years (mA)). The Sijes Formation is represented by 

sandstone, silt, clay, tuff and evaporite such as halite, gypsum, borate and travertine. Its 

sedimentary thickness is over 400 meters.   

 

Over a long period of time, evapo-concentration of surface water resulted in saturated 

salt brines rich in lithium, potassium and sodium chlorides. Mineralization at the Property 

includes lithium-potassium rich – relatively low magnesium brine as detected through surface 

sampling and drilling. The brine can be classified as a sulphate-chloride type with anomalous 

lithium. Lithium concentrations at the surface at Pastos Grande Salar range from about 100-



3 

 

300 milligrams per liter (mg/L). Lithium concentrations increase with depth, with holes as 

shallow as 10 meters reporting as much as 400 mg/L.  A value of 578 mg/L was reported at a 

depth of 30 meters in borehole DW01PG.  Average lithium concentration is about 450 mg/L 

for the samples obtained to date and used to estimate the mineral resource. 

1.4  TECHNICAL PROGRAMS 
 

The Property exhibits elevated lithium and potassium brines and has been tested by 

surface geochemical sampling, Controlled Source Audio-frequency Magnetotelluric 

(CSAMT) surveying, Vertical Electrical Sounding (VES) geophysical surveying, diamond 

coring of boreholes, mud rotary drilling of wells, depth-specific brine and core sampling, and 

pumping tests at completed wells. 

 

Prior to Millennial Lithium's work, the Pastos Grandes Salar had been explored by 

different companies between 2007-2012, in exploration programs that included surface 

sampling, geophysics, and drilling. The main effort in the Property was a 2-year, Phase I 

exploration program by Eramine Sudamerica S.A. (Eramine) during 2011-2012; results were 

considered by Millennial to be favorable for continued exploration. 

 

Recent Phase II drilling by Millennial has continued to demonstrate the extent of good 

quality brine in the salar and also indicates that lithium-enriched brine is also found in a large 

thickness of clastic sediments below the previous maximum depth drilled of 160 meters.  The 

current deepest depth-specific brine sample is from a depth of 593 meters at Millennial well 

PGMW17-05c, showing a lithium content of 539 mg/L. Exploration drilling activities by 

Millennial in support of resource characterization started in 2016 and were completed in 

November 2017, and included the following: 

 

• Multiple exploration coreholes drilled at 11 locations to depths ranging from 154 to 
601 meters, 

• Total drilling of 4,010 meters of exploration drilling at the seven polygons used to 
estimate the Measured, Indicated, and Inferred resources, 

• Collection and analysis of 165 depth-specific brine samples, not including duplicates, 
with an average spacing of about 25 meters,  



4 

 

• Analysis of 108 depth-specific core samples and repacked sediment samples for 
drainable and total porosity testing, with an average spacing of about 40 meters, 

• One pumping test well, with a total cased depth of 348 meters, and  
• A 60-hour pumping test at well PGPW16-01 with collection of 20 brine samples during 

pumping. 
 

One of the basic chemical relationships indicative of lithium brine quality is 

magnesium (Mg) to lithium (Li) (Mg:Li). The quality of the Pastos Grandes brine is similar to 

that in the Salar de Atacama. Potassium to lithium ratio (K:Li) is similar to Hombre Muerto, 

suggesting that a conventional circuit could produce approximately four times as much 

commercial grade potassium salts as LCE.  The following table presents averages of lithium 

and magnesium contents from analyzed samples, and Mg:Li ratio from Phase II drilling and 

testing.

 As of the writing of this report, exploration well PGPW17-02 is being drilled at the 

PGMW-04 location.  This well will be suitable for aquifer testing and is the first Phase III well 

of the new 2017/2018 program designed to move toward development of a Mineral Reserve. 



5 

 

1.5  CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
  

The Phase II exploration program was designed to estimate the lithium and potassium 

content at Millennial Lithium Corporation's Pastos Grandes project to accepted standards.  The 

outcome of 8 months of drilling and testing was an estimated measured and indicated resource 

of 400,000 tonnes of lithium, and 4.27 million tonnes of potassium.

The reader is cautioned that mineral resources are not mineral reserves and do not have demonstrated 
economic viability. 
 

Additional areas to be further defined include inferred resources of 165,000 tonnes of lithium, 

and 1.7 million tonnes of KCl equivalent.  

 

 It is common and accepted practice with such brine resource is to report lithium as 

Li2CO3 Lithium Carbonate Equivalent (LCE), and to report potassium as KCl equivalent.  

After doing the conversions, the equivalent measured and indicated resources are 2.1 million 

tonnes LCE, and 8.1 million tonnes of potassium chloride.

Additional areas to be further defined contain inferred resources of 0.9 million tonnes 

of LCE, and 3.3 million tonnes of KCl equivalent.  

 

The author recommends advancing the project to the feasibility stage to include further 

drilling and pumping tests in order to determine production pumping sustainability and to 

derive the data needed for production well planning and a hydrogeological numeral model to 

be used, in conjunction with feasibility-level engineering and commercial studies, to develop 

a future mineral reserve. 

 

Over a period of approximately 24 months, the budget for Phase III exploration and the 

ongoing Preliminary Economic Assessment (PEA) through feasibility, is estimated at US$23 

million, with the approximately US$10 million designated for engineering and engineering-

related studies, and US$5.6 million designated for resource and hydrogeological works. 

1.6  AUTHOR'S STATEMENT AND QUALIFICATIONS 
 

Michael Rosko of Montgomery & Associates Consultores Limitada (Montgomery & 

Associates) takes responsibility for the results of the 2016/2017 exploration program and 

associated analyses, and assumes responsibility for all sections of the current report. Mr. Rosko 

is a Registered member of Society for Mining, Metallurgy, and Exploration (SME), and has 

many years of experience designing and evaluating lithium brine exploration and development 

projects. Michael Rosko is a QP as defined under the NI 43-101 definition of a QP, and is 



7 

 

independent of Millennial.  The author visited the Pastos Grandes Property on December 9 and 

10, 2016, May 5 and 6, 2017, and previously in April, 2012 when Eramine was conducting 

pumping tests. The objectives of the site visits were as follows: 

 

• To become familiar with the layout and scale of the Pastos Grandes Property,  
• To view sampling and drill hole locations,  
• To review core samples and drill cuttings, 
• To inspect drilling operations and sampling protocols, 
• To evaluate access for future drilling locations, and 
• Do a preliminary inspection of potential sources of fresh water.

2.  INTRODUCTION 
 

Lithium and potassium in Salar de Pastos Grandes were first studied by the Dirección 

General de Fabricaciones Militares (DGFM) who completed extensive exploration in the 

Argentinean salars in the 1970s. This governmental effort resulted in the definition of the Salar 

de Hombre Muerto West, where in 1997, the Mina Fénix operation was started by FMC 

Lithium.  A new wave of exploration for lithium began between 2007 - 2010, when 

international mining explorers and developers utilized exploration techniques including 

surface geochemistry, geophysical studies and exploration drilling at numerous basins in the 

region, including: Salar de Cauchari/Olaroz, Salinas Grandes, Salars de Centenario and 

Ratones, Salar del Rincon, Salar de Pastos Grandes, Salar de Pozuelos, Salar de Diablillos, 

Salar del Hombre Muerto East, and Salar de Llullaillaco. In early 2016, new companies and 

various joint ventures showed renewed interest in the already known salars and other less 

explored areas, including Salar de Arizaro, Salar de Incahuasi, Salar de Pocitos, Salar de Rio 

Grande, Salar de Antofalla, the Carrachi Pampa basin, Salar de Jama, Laguna de Vilama, and 

the Laguna de Guayatayoc basin. 

 

The Millennial Lithium Corporation (Millennial) Properties are located in the central 

portion of the Salar de los Pastos Grandes basin, within the Argentinean portion of the Lithium 

Triangle of South America Argentina-Bolivia-Chile (Figure 4.1). Millennial's main interest is 

to define and develop facilities to produce lithium chemical compounds, as well as other 

commodities amenable to extraction during the concentration processes (ie. potassium, boron, 

etc.).  Studies have previously demonstrated the presence of commercial lithium and potassium 

brine concentrations, but have not been able to estimate the resource amount, nor the economic 

viability of commercial extraction.   

 

The present Report was prepared for Millennial by Montgomery and Associates to 

document Phase II exploration work and to estimate lithium and potassium resources on the 

Millennial mining properties registered at Salta Mining Court, namely the Jorge Eduardo mina 

(File 18693); Neptalí II (File 18403); El Milagro (File 17588); Norte Argentino (File 18,550), 

and Papadopoulos XXXII lease (File 19667) Taba PG (File 20016); Papadopoulos LXXIV 

(File 20247), and; Aguamarca XV (File 19097), of the Los Andes Department, Province of 



9 

 

Salta, Northwestern Argentina (the "Property").  Although the Recursos Energeticos y Mineros 

Salta S.A. (REMSA) XIII properties (File 22765) are currently in control by Millennial, Phase 

II exploration was not conducted on these holdings because the final acquisition papers were 

not signed until December 2017, and post-dated the exploration work.

4.  PROPERTY LOCATION AND DESCRIPTION 
 
 

4.1  PROPERTY LOCATION 
 

The Property is in the central portion of the Salar de Pastos Grandes Basin, and is 

located in the Los Andes Department, in the Central portion of the Puna Block of Salta 

Province, northwestern Argentina (Figure 4.1), 13 kilometers (km) south–southeast of the 

Santa Rosa de los Pastos Grandes village, 56 km southwest of the regional administrative 

centre at San Antonio de los Cobres and 154 km west-northwest of the Salta provincial capital.  

The Property is 388 km straight line distant east-south-east of Antofagasta, Chile, the closest 

sea port.  The central coordinates of the Salar are 24°34'44" south latitude and 66°42'26" west 

longitude, at an average elevation of 3,785 meters above sea level (masl).  

 
Figure 4.1. Location of Salar de Pastos Grandes 

 



12 

 

 

4.2  PROPERTY DESCRIPTION 
 

The present surface expression of the Salar de Pastos Grandes is a roughly-triangular 

surface with a 9 km north-south baseline with the apex 7 km to the east. The salt pan is almost 

completely flat with the exception of remains of remnant salar surface standing up to 15 meters 

over the central part of the Salar (Figure 4.2).  

 

Millennial controls 8,664 hectares as mining licenses on and around the Pastos Grandes 

salar.  The initial mining properties acquired by Millennial included the Jorge Eduardo, Neptali 

2, El Milagro, Norte Argentino, and Papadopoulos XXXII minas (Table 4.1). With the 

exception of the Papadopoulos XXXII mina, the areas were previously registered to Jorge 

Moreno and Alba Salas, a family from Salta maintaining the mining rights in the area and 

producing halite (common salt), for the last 10 years. These were optioned to, and subsequently 

acquired by, Millennial Lithium through their local wholly-owned subsidiary, Proyecto Pastos 

Grandes, S.A.. The properties are irregularly shaped and cover a surface area of 1,519.4 ha. 

Most of the properties lie on Salar crust, the northern limit of which is peripheral to the 

northwest portion of the Salar (Figure 4.2). An additional 4,652.95 ha were subsequently 

added (Taba PG; Papadopoulos LXXIV, and; Aguamarca 15), with final concessions granted 

in October 2016. 

 

An additional 2,492.05 ha were awarded in August 2017 in a tender held by the 

Province-owned resources and energy distribution company REMSA, with the final contract 

signed in December 2017.

4.3  TYPE OF MINERAL TENURE 
 

According to Argentine Law, mineral resources belong to the provinces where the 

resource is located. Each province has the authority to grant exploration permits and 

exploitation concession rights to private applicant entities.  However, the Federal Congress is 

entitled to enact the National Mining Code and any substantive mining legislation which is 

similarly applicable in all of the country. Provinces have the authority to regulate the 

procedural aspects of the National Mining Code and to organize each enforcement authority 

within its territory. 

 

In general, there are two types of mining rights that can be granted under Argentinean 

mining law: 

 

• Exploration Permits (usually refer to as "Cateos") that are limited in time and have 

limited obligations; and 

• Exploitation Concessions (usually refer to as "Minas" or "Claims") that are unlimited 

in time as long as obligations set out in the National Mining Code are met by the title 

holders. 

 

All concessions are granted by the regulating province either by a judicial (Salta) or 

administrative decision, depending on the province.  An Exploration Permit can be transformed 

into a Mining Permit any time before the expiry date of the Exploration Permit by presenting 

a report and paying a canon (rent). Tenure for exploitation concessions is indefinite providing 

that semi-annual payments are made in February and July each year. 

 

In the Salta province, all concessions are granted by a judge in the Mining Court. Each 

property is recorded by number in the Mining Court registry, and each property has its own 

judicial file. In addition, the Mining Secretariat records the property in the Registro Gráfico 

("Land Register Office") and adds the property to a digital map of the area.  The claims that 

make up the Pastos Grandes Property are "minas", or mining permits, and therefore are 

unlimited in duration, provided that the biannual rents are paid each year.  

 



16 

 

4.4  INTEREST IN PROPERTY 
 

Pursuant to the Purchase Agreement, the Company acquired a 100% interest in the 

original Moreno Group Pastos Grandes Properties by making the following payments and 

share issuances: 

 

▪ a $200,000 USD deposit (which has been paid);  
▪ a payment of $500,000 USD and $500,000 USD in common shares of the Company on 

Exchange approval of a definitive agreement with the Moreno Group (completed); 
▪ a payment of $500,000 USD and $500,000 USD in common shares of the Company on 

the 6-month anniversary of Exchange approval of the definitive agreement with the 
Moreno Group (completed); 

▪ a payment of $1,000,000 USD on the 1-year anniversary of Exchange approval of the 
definitive agreement (completed); and 

▪ granting a 1.5% net smelter return royalty, which can be bought by Millennial for a 
one-time payment of $3 million USD. 

 

The 300-hectare Papadopoulos XXXII mina was declared vacant by the mining 

court and opened to claiming by paying back-rents and a lottery process, won by 

Millennial's subsidiary, Proyecto Pastos Grandes S.A. The Papadopoulos LXXIV, 

Aguamarca XV and Taba PG minas, were vacant and open during the period that Proyecto 

Pastos Grandes S.A. was in the process of being incorporated, and were thus obtained by 

Millennial's previous consulting group, Rojas and Associates, and were later transferred to 

the company. The license group is collectively referred to in the report as the "Property" 

and the previous owners as the "Vendors". 
 

The most recent addition to the Company's holdings at Pastos Grandes was the 2,492.05 ha 

Special Investigation Area put out for a competitive tender by REMSA. The tender was 

awarded to Millennial, and the REMSA XIII properties (File 22765) were fully in control by 

Millennial as of December 2017.  Provincial code states that when mining properties are 

released, or are forfeited for lack of payment or filing requirements, REMSA has the right of 

first refusal to acquire the area, provided that within 2 years they release each particular 

property or collections of properties within investigation areas for sale, lease or partnership. 

The REMSA XIII properties were released in June 2017, and subject to a detailed set of 

terms, including applicant qualifications, require a first-year work commitment, initial 



17 

 

payment and overall payment. The starting date for the work commitment is coincident with 

the issuance of the Salta Provincial environmental approval. 

 

Due to intense competition for this strategic area, Millennial offered US$3,000 per 

hectare, for a total US$7,476,150 (CAD$9.4M) and a first-year (Stage I) work commitment 

of US$15.44M (C$19.4M). The payments are to be divided into four equal payments of 

US$1,869,037.50 (C$2.35M), the first due on signing the contract with REMSA and receipt  

of the titles for properties contained within the investigation area from the Salta Mining Court. 

Details are summarized below.   
 

• Offer/ha: 3,000 USD 
• Area (ha): 2,492.05 
• Total offer: 7,476,150.00 USD 
• Initial payment: 1,869,037.50 USD (25% on contract signing) 
• Second payment: 1,869,037.50 USD (25% on first anniversary of signing) 
• Third payment: 1,869,037.50 USD (25% on second anniversary of signing) 
• Fourth payment: 1,869,037.50 USD (25% on third anniversary of signing) 

 

4.5  ROYALTIES AND OTHER INTERESTS 
 

The Company granted the Moreno Group a royalty of 1.5% of the gross operating 

revenues (GOR) from production from their licenses once a lithium brine project enters 

production. The Moreno Group has granted the Company the right to buy back the royalty for 

payment of $3 million USD. 

 

Once a lithium brine project enters production in the Province of Salta, royalties of 3% 

over net revenue are paid directly to the Province. Such royalty is referred to as "boca mina," 

or mouth-of-mine. The boca mina value is calculated as sales price minus all costs for 

processing, handling, transport, insurance, and tariffs. 

 



18 

 

As the mining licenses have been specified for lithium, the Company is required to pay 

a bi-annual canon rent on the Property of $1,600 Argentinean Pesos (approximately $88 USD) 

per 100 ha. 

 

4.6  OPERATING PERMITS 
 

The permitting process as proscribed by the Mining Law of Argentina is very 

straightforward in Salta. The permit to mine is granted as part of the mining license but 

environmental approval is required by the office of the Mining Secretary for the Province of 

Salta.  This authorization is obtained by filing an Environmental Impact Report.  

 

The contents of these reports will vary according to the type and stage of activity being 

carried out on the property. The information requested is submitted as an extraction permit, 

covering quarries, water and brine. The areas to be addressed, as requested by the Mining 

Secretary are: 

• Statement of the company's legal address in Salta. 

• The nature of the contractual agreement between the company applying and the owner, 

• Submission of the form "Solicitud de Cantera" (Request for a quarry), 

• Submission of a form stating that the company is debt free, and 

• The drilling schedule. 

  

Millennial submitted the initial Environmental Impact Report and received approval 

for exploration in October, 2016.  The Environmental Impact Report must be renewed every 2 

years, or when there is a change in activity expected to occur on the property.  Addenda were 

subsequently filled in 2017 to include additional drill sites and pilot test evaporations ponds. 

 

The original Property acquisition was subjected to a thorough legal due diligence which 

revealed no other significant factors and risks that may affect access, title or right and the 

ability to work on the property.   



19

5.1 ACCESSIBILITY 
 

The Project area and roads are shown on Figure 5.1.  Access from Salta is via National 

Route 51 (RN-51) 170 km west and northwest to San Antonio de los Cobres.  From San 

Antonio de los Cobres, the route is 15 km along RN-51 to the split of Provincial Route 129 

(RP- 127) and from there 50 km toward Santa Rosa de los Pastos Grandes village. The project 

camp facilities are located 11 km south of the village. 

 

 
Figure 5.1.  Road Map of Property Area 

 

From Antofagasta, Chile, the access is via the Panamerican Highway 5N 70 km to 

Baquedano, proceeding east along Routes 365, 367 and 23 for some 300 km to the Sico 

international pass between Chile and Argentina. From Sico, the shorter access to the project 

site is 130 km via routes RN-51, RP-127 and RP-129 through Cauchari and Pocitos. 



20 

 

 

5.2 CLIMATE 
 

Climate conditions in the high altitude Puna region are characterized by extremely dry, 

windy, low temperature and oxygen-poor air. The average annual rainfall decreases 

significantly from east to west and from north to south. For example, rainfall averages 115 

millimeters (mm) at San Antonio of Cobres (coordinates S24.2232°, W66.3192°), while it is 

just 32 mm in Unquillal Railroad Station (coordinates S24.535729, W67.205349°).  In the 

areas of San Antonio de los Cobres and Salar del Hombre Muerto, records indicate winter 

precipitations, mainly as snow and hail, but in small quantities. Snow in the Puna region does 

not accumulate on the ground for long, due to the high evaporation, the extreme dryness of the 

air and constant strong winds. Nevertheless, in places strong wind accumulates snow in low 

areas and roads cuts making access by vehicular traffic sometimes difficult during winter. 

These accumulations become stronger and more frequent at higher elevation (over 4,200 masl). 

 

Rainfall during the mid-summer months can sometimes disrupt exploration activities. 

Local rainfall in this region of the Puna averages 70 mm per year. In dry years, the rain does 

not significantly impact exploration activities. In wet years, such as was the case in 2016/2017 

the rain can be heavy enough to inundate salars with water. During those years, elevated 

roadways might be required to access exploration or operations sites within salar boundaries. 

Disruptive snowfall is not common in the region, but is known to occur. 

 

Net annual evaporation (evaporation minus precipitation) in this region of the puna is 

typically 2,500 mm per year, with evaporation rates peaking mid-spring to early summer, and 

late summer to mid-autumn.  Solar evaporation slows in the cooler winter months, and can be 

locally offset by rain during the mid-summer months. 

 

Wide diurnal temperature differences are common during both summer and winter. 

Thermal amplitudes of 30 degrees Celsius (°C) to 35°C are common, and are more pronounced 

during the winter months. 



21 

 

5.2.1  Meteorological Data Sources 
 

Climate data have been collected within and around the Pastos Grandes salar watershed 

area by several organizations. Relevant and available stations can be grouped into two general 

categories: 

▪ Off-site. Operated in San Antonio de los Cobres town and in Hombre Muerto Salar 
(Tincalayu mine), by the Argentinean National Weather Service (Servicio 

Meteorologico Nacional - SMN); and 

▪ On-site. Operated within the salar by Eramine during 2012 and 2013. 
 

5.2.2  San Antonio de los Cobres Weather Station 
 

Located at Latitude: S24.2208, Longitude: W66.3162 and an altitude of 3,770 masl. 

Climate parameters are as follows: 

Rainfall: The annual average rainfall is 103.9 mm with the highest occurrence in the 

period January to March. The annual average maximum recorded is 144 mm. 

Temperature: The annual average temperature is 8.6°C. The period from November 

to February has the highest monthly average and is on the order of 12°C; the coldest month is 

July with an average temperature of 2.2°C. The maximum and minimum measured 

temperatures are 27°C and -21°C, respectively.  

Humidity: The average annual relative humidity is 43.8%. Monthly average maximum 

humidity is in July at 58%; the minimum is in October at 34%.  

Frost: The average annual frequency of frost days is 223, most frequently between 

April and October. 

Wind: The wind tends to be very strong in the Puna, with gusts as high as 100 

kilometers per hour (kph), usually occurring between noon and dusk. The strongest winds are 

recorded in the months of July and August. When the wind coincides with heavy snow, white-

outs (locally referred to as "White Wind") can cause stoppages in mining work and the 

movement of people and vehicles. The prevailing wind direction is from the northwest 

quadrant, shifting southerly during the mid-summer months. Wind speed and wind direction 

are shown on Figure 5.2. 



22 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

Figure 5.2.  Wind Speed and Direction in the Region 
 

5.2.3  Hombre Muerto Weather Station 
 

The Hombre Muerto weather station is located at the Tincalayu Mine in the northwest 

corner of the salar (S25.2715, W67.0509), at an altitude of 4,010 masl. Climate parameters are 

as follows: 

Rainfall: The average annual rainfall is 63.8 mm, with the monthly highest rainfall 

being in January with 31.4 mm.  During August through November, rainfall is rare. In the 

Hombre Muerto Salar, snowfall occurs occasionally in the winter months and rainfall between 

November and early March. 

Temperature: The average annual temperature is 4.7°C. The warmest months are 

January and February with an average monthly temperature of 10.9°C and 10.3°C, 

respectively. The coldest month is July with a monthly average temperature of -28°C. The 

maximum and minimum average annual temperatures are 13.9°C and -4 °C, respectively. The 

absolute minimum temperature in January is -10°C and -28°C in July, while the absolute 

maximum temperature is 26°C and 12°C, respectively. 

Frost: The average annual frequency of days with frost is on the order of 350 days. 

Humidity: There is no data for humidity or wind from the Hombre Muerto station. 



23

5.3  LOCAL RESOURCES 
 

No significant resources are available at the Property itself, although basic first aid, 

accommodation and food can be found at the village of Santa Rosa de los Pastos Grandes. 

 

5.4  INFRASTRUCTURE IN THE REGION 
 

No local production of basic domestic or industrial goods is found at the Property or 

neighboring areas.  Most of the provisions needed are brought from Salta or San Antonio de 

los Cobres. The village at Santa Rosa de Los Pastos Grandes, with 120 inhabitants and located 

12 km north of the Property, provides basic infrastructure including a domestic water system 

and diesel based power generation of 220 volts, a first aid station with ambulance service, and 

limited, basic hosting and restaurant services. The town of San Antonio de Los Cobres, 65 km 

and a 1 hour drive from Santa Rosa, is the center of the Department of Los Andes and has a 

population of approximately 6,000 people. It is an active commercial and tourist center with 

several hotels, and a number of restaurants that provide good support for explorers in the 

region. Salar de Pocitos village is home to about 100 inhabitants, and located 36 km northwest 

of the Property; the village is a station on the Antofagasta-Salta international railway. The 

terminus of the Puna Gas Pipeline includes an extension for the Mina Fenix (lithium) operation 

at Salar del Hombre Muerto, and possibly for the Tincalayu borate operations in the future. 



26 

 

 

Major infrastructure is linked to San Antonio de los Cobres and Salta with connections 

to Chile and other communities of the Puna region, as shown in Figures 5.1 and 5.3. 

 
 Figure 5.3.  Infrastructure Map for the Pastos Grandes Region  
 

5.4.1  Road Connections 
 

National Route RN-51, passing through San Antonio de los Cobres connects Salta city 

in Argentina with Antofagasta city in Chile on the Pacific coast. RN-51 in the Salta – San 

Antonio de los Cobres segment (170 km) is more than 70% paved. From San Antonio de los 

Cobres, National Route RN-51 leads to the international border at the Sico Pass, consisting of 

140 km of well-maintained gravel road. From the Sico Pass, Chilean routes 23, 367 and 365 

connect the 300 km to Antofagasta, the most populated city in northern Chile and the largest 

sea harbor in the region. 

 



27 

 

PR-129, a well-maintained dirt road, connects the Salar de Pastos Grandes with San 

Antonio de los Cobres and the Salar de Pocitos village (Figures 5.1 and 5.3). 

5.4.2  Electrical Power Connection 
 

The 600 megawatt (MW), 375 kilovolt (KV) power line between Salta and Mejillones 

in Chile passes 53 km to the north of the Property. The line was built with the aim to transport 

energy from Argentina to Chile, but had been out of service since 2009. In February, 2016, the 

line resumed operation and transports 110 MW from Mejillones (Chile) to the Argentinean 

Interconnected System. In the event that a power supply to Pastos Grandes is required, a 

transformer station and a power line of 60 km could be installed off the main line. 

5.4.3  Natural Gas 
 

A natural gas line (Gas de la Puna) passes through San Antonio de los Cobres to 

Estacion Salar de Pocitos, 26 km northwest of the Property. At Estacion Salar de Pocitos there 

is an industrial park where a gas distribution pipeline feeds the Mina Fenix (at Salar del 

Hombre Muerto) and other operations in the Puna being developed. This is also another 

alternative power supply for a potential lithium operation at Salar de Pastos Grandes.  

5.4.4  Railway Antofagasta-Salta 
 

An existing railroad between Salta and Antofagasta is administrated by two different 

companies: The Chilean Ferrocarril Antofagasta – Bolivia (Luksic Group) and the Argentinean 

state owned Ferrocarril Belgrano. It consists of a narrow-gauge railway connecting 

Antofagasta (Chile) on the Pacific coast to the northern part of Argentina with connections to 

Buenos Aires on the Atlantic coast. It is presently out of service through the Puna area, 

although temporarily maintained in both country segments. The permanent reactivation of this 

connection is under way in cooperation between the regional governments. The Chilean 

portion has worked hauling copper cathodes and providing general supply for the Escondida 

and Zaldivar mines (in Chile). More recently, it has worked intermittently transporting borates, 

fruit, cattle and grains between Salta and Antofagasta. Transportation costs to the Pacific coast 



28 

 

and the port of Antofagasta using this link would benefit a lithium operation at Pastos Grandes 

and elsewhere in the Puna. 

5.5  PHYSIOGRAPHY 

5.5.1  Topography 
 

The present-day salar comprises an area of some 36 square kilometers (km2) of mostly 

flat sandy-silty salt crust. The flat, salt crust surface is disrupted over approximately 15 percent 

of its area by elevated outcrops of a coarser sand-silt mix. Some have interpreted these outcrops 

to comprise slightly older salar sediments that were eroded, yet remained as more resistant 

"islands". This interpretation is supported by the latest drilling interpretations. 

 

Millennial's Properties are sufficiently large enough to host evaporation ponds for the 

envisioned 25 kilotonnes per annum (ktpa) plant. While pond sizing has yet to be determined, 

a 25 ktpa lithium operation can be anticipated to require approximately 9 to 11 km2 of total 

ponds area, including liming ponds, halite concentration ponds, potash ponds and final lithium 

"cook up" ponds. The areas covered by minas controlled by Millennial are in themselves 

probably sufficient to contain the required ponds, but in the case additional area is required, it 

is a straightforward process to apply for a "servidumbre," or easement for adjacent areas related 

to infrastructure and operations. 



29 

 

 

Figure 5.4.  General Topography of the Pastos Grandes Area 



30 

 

 

5.5.2  Soils 
 

Soil development in the Puna area is sparse, and classified as skeletal soils of aridisol 

type. Characteristics of these soils include them being ocher in color, with very low organic 

matter, low fertility and a relatively coarse texture.  According to the map of the soils of Nadir 

and Chafatinos (1990), in the area of the Pastos Grandes Salar, soils are specifically 

characterized as lithosols of the Earth Group "E". Lithosols are a type of azonal soil consisting 

chiefly or partly of weathered rock fragments that are typically found on steep slopes and have 

no economic value. There is a narrow zone of soil at the outer edge of the Pastos Grandes Salar, 

but within the salar, surface soils are crusted salt or solid halite. 

 

According to the Argentina Geological Mining Service (SEGEMAR) classification, the 

Pastos Grandes Salar has a saline soil type "La". The ground surrounding the salar, where the 

dunes and wetlands are located, is classified as DGtc-7 soil type. Soils in the remaining 

territory where consolidated rock crops out, and on natural elevations, are classified as EKtc-

14 and ENi -6 soils. 

 

5.5.3 Air Quality 
 

Air at the elevation of Salar de Pastos Grandes (3,780 masl) is dry, with average 30-

40% humidity, and low oxygen levels.  Wind is intense in the winter season, especially in the 

afternoons, typically decreasing at night and morning. Dust and sand storms occur 

occasionally, at times lasting several days.  During the remainder of the year the wind is less 

intense and with little suspended material. In general, air quality in Pastos Grandes is good. 

 

 

  



31 

 

5.6  BIOSPHERE (VEGETATION AND FAUNA) 
 

From a biogeographical point of view, the Salar de Pastos Grandes has characteristics 

of both the "Puneña" and "Altoandina" provinces, both belonging to the Andean Patagonian 

domain within the Neotropical Region (Cabrera and Willink, 1980). 

 

5.6.1  Vegetation 
 

Although typical vegetation in the Puna area consists of sparse, low shrub steppe-type 

vegetation, except for the northern-most part, the Property is without vegetation, being covered 

with unvegetated saline crust.  Excessive cold, wind and the lack of water are some of the 

features of the severe climatic conditions, which bring a poor regional vegetation of xerophile 

and halophile types.  

 

The "Altoandina province" is situated above 4,200 masl and exhibits a cold climate all 

year. Precipitation is typically snow or hail. Winds are variable and can be intense. Vegetation 

coverage of the surface is less than 5% and comprises herbaceous growth of low to medium 

height, characterized by Poaceae family as Deyeuxia Vulva ("Iru"), D. Tristoides ("cola de 

zorro"), Digitaria Californica ("pasto puna"), Diplachne Dubia ("cortadera") and Distichlis 

Humilis ("pasto del salitral"). 

 

The "Punena province" is between 3,200 and 4,000 masl and is characterized by dry 

and cold weather with seasonal variations in temperature being less than daily. Precipitation 

occurs between November and April and decreases from east to west and north to south. 

 

In the humid Puna places (as Salar de Pastos Grandes) the landscape is dominated by 

stipa and fescue dolichophila grasses. In the highest areas of the Puna, vegetation is short 

having adapted to the radiation, dryness, strong winds and severe cooling temperatures. 

 

Over the driest areas of the Puna, scattered grasses and low shrubs occur and include: 

Fabiana sp, Adesmia sp, Parastrephia sp, Bacharis sp, Maihuenopsis and Polylepis sp 



32 

 

tomentela (endangered), Ferozerable Prosopis (used as firewood), Trichosereus pascana 

(endangered and used in construction), Larrea divaricata ("Jarilla hembra"), Artemisia 

vulgaris ("Ajenjo"), Haplopapus rigidus (locally "Bailabuena" and endangered due to 

medicinal use), Alcantholippia deserticola phil (locally "rica rica" and endangered due to 

medicinal use), Baccharis incarum ("Tola"), and Senecio eriophyton or Escalonia Resinosa 

("Chachacoma").  Currently, these plant communities are altered due to the use of locals and 

overgrazing by domestic cattle.  

5.6.2  Fauna 
 

The fauna of the Puna is characterized by its adaptation to extreme living conditions as 

a result of the great aridity, intense sunlight during the day and very low temperatures at night. 

Many animals have nocturnal habits, and live protected under rocks or in cracks. Others live 

buried or acquire certain physiological behavior allowing them to withstand the harsh 

environment in which they live. 

 

Cabrera and Willink (1980) describe the animal species in the Puneña biogeographic 

province. In the Pastos Grandes area, camelids such as vicuña (Vicugna vicugna) and llama 

(Lama glama), the latter domesticated, exist. Fox occur and (Dusicyon, Lycalopex) represent 

a carnivorous species that occurs in the area.  Among rodents common to the area, a mole, 

named Oculto or Tuco-Tuco (Ctenomys opimus) contributes to desertification of large areas as 

it feeds on roots of local flora. Additionally, the Puna mouse (Auliscomys sublimis) lives in the 

region. 

 

Birds in the region include the Parina or Andean flamingo, living in moist and salty 

lagoons known as the Cerceta de la Puna (Anas Puna), and the Andean Goose, Guayata or 

Huallata (Chloephaga melanoptera). The queu or quevo (Tinamotis pentlandi) inhabits the 

highlands and is similar to a large partridge. The Nandu enano (Rhea) comparable with the 

species Pterocnemia pennata (its classification is questioned) inhabits the lower plains of the 

region. Small parrots, pigeons and owls also exist as sporadic inhabitants.  The donkey 

(donEquus africanus asinuskey) is a species introduced by inhabitants of the area. Although 

domesticated, it competes for food with llamas and vicunas. 



33 

 

5.7  HYDROSPHERE 
 

A large portion of northwestern Argentina lies within the area of endorheic basins 

region and geologically belongs to the Puna Geological Province (Turner, 1972) and the Sub-

Province of Puna Austral (Alonso et al., 1984). All waters in the Puna environment typically 

drain to closed or centripetal basins where they accumulate or evaporate. The Salar de Pastos 

Grandes lies in the Puna Region-Puna Austral region. From the hydrogeological point of view 

it corresponds to the Mountainous Front Province, according to the criterion of their origin 

(Issar and Passchier, 1990).  Low rainfall rates registered in the area and high insolation 

produce strong evaporation potential (5 to 8 millimeters per day (mm/day)) resulting in a 

condition of intense dryness. The result is a net evaporation for the area and salt deposition 

occurring due to evaporation of surface water.   

 

The Salar de Pastos Grandes is a closed basin and the basin floor has an elevation of 

3,785 masl. The basin is fed by streams coming from the northeast (Pastos Grandes and Corral 

Colorado rivers), east (Arroyo Sijes), and in a lesser extent from northwest (Cóndor Huasi 

marsh and stream) which drains water from the El Azufre mountain.  The outstanding 

geomorphologic - hydrological features of this basin, include:  

 

a) The alluvial cone developed by the Corral Colorado and Pastos Grandes rivers.  Both 

rivers are permanent and located in the northern sector of the basin between the Nevados de 

Pastos Grandes to the west and Sierra de Pastos Grandes to the east. The origin of the waters 

of both rivers is from melting of snow in the high peaks; and 

  

b) The Sijes River which flows to the southeast edge of the Salar and collects the waters 

of the Ochaqui Creek and numerous others tributaries of the western slope of the Sierra de 

Quilmes. The water is mostly originates from the melting of snow on the high peaks.  

 

The Pastos Grandes River, upstream of the village, includes a broad plain with several 

meanders, and has been estimated to flow at a rate of about 20 liters per second (L/s). 

Downstream of the village the river infiltrates and resurfaces about 1,500 meters downgradient.   



34 

 

6.  HISTORY 
 
 

Borax Argentina has mined borates (colemanite - hydroboracite - ulexite), on the 

southern and eastern portions of Pastos Grandes Salar since the early 1960's. These minerals 

are processed at the Sijes borates plant, presently operated by the local subsidiary of Orocobre 

Limited.  

 

In 1979, the DGFM conducted an exploration program covering a number of salars in 

the Puna Region.  This study included Pastos Grandes, where their work included mapping 

and surface sampling comprising collecting six brine samples from the surface, eight from 

hand-dug pits, and another four from streams feeding the basin. The samples from the salar 

were consistent, returning average values of 384 parts per million (ppm) Li and 4,066 ppm K 

for the pit samples, and 327 ppm Li and 3,518 ppm K for the surface samples.  Not surprisingly, 

most of the stream samples reported Li values below the detection limits. 

 

In 1987, Ulex established borate operations on the southeastern extension of the Pastos 

Grandes basin at the Sol de Mañana mine, producing some 1,000 tonnes of colemanite-

hydroboracite-ulexite per year.  Tramo SRL has mined borates (colemanite) at the Quebracho 

property on the southern border of the Pastos Grandes salar and common salt (halite, NaCl) on 

the salar surface since 2006. Other minor mining groups have also carried out salt exploitation 

over various properties in the Salar. 

 

During 2011 and 2012, Eramine studied part of the Pastos Grandes Salar and carried 

out drilling, testing, and geochemical sampling, including at five locations in the current 

Millennial holdings. This work included sampling of sub-surficial brines and a drilling 

program limited to maximum depth of 160 meters.  Eramine also carried out geophysical 

surveys including seismic, CSAMT and VES.  Results from the VES in many areas of the salar 

were interpreted as consisting of thick halite layers.  

 

 



35 

 

7.  GEOLOGICAL SETTING AND MINERALIZATION 
 

7.1  REGIONAL GEOLOGY 
 

The Pastos Grandes Salar sits in the vicinity of the intersection of two main geological 

features consisting of north-south structurally-controlled basins and northwest magmatic-

structural corridors. The intersection between these structural units is a significant influence in 

the evolution of the western portion of the Central Andes of South America. The north-south 

(grading to north-northeast) structures exhibit in general periods of normal and reverse 

faulting, whereas the north-west structures are characterized by reverse and dextral offsets 

(Coira et al., 1982; Marret et al., 2005; Allmendinger et al. 1997; Chernicoff et al., 2002). 

 

These structural features are of regional-continental scale, and were developed inland 

on the western continental margin at least from middle Paleozoic time. Periods of compression 

and relaxation are characterized by reverse and normal faulting, forming a succession of basin 

and range style morphology along the continental back-arc regions. This main set of structures 

and especially those of northwest orientation, repeats along intervals of 80-150 km (Figure 

7.1).  These elevated enclosed basins hosted high altitude seas and lakes that evolved as 

extended inland depressions. The intersection of the north-south and north-west main features 

and other northeast structures generated weak spots where magmatism and hydrothermal 

activity has occurred since the Cretaceous period.  

 



36 

 

 

Figure 7.1.  Structural Setting of the Puna Region  

 

In the Pastos Grandes region several parallel north-south structures intercept the 

Calama-Olacapato-Toro lineament carrying Upper Miocene-Oligocene acidic-intermediate 

volcanism and the development of huge volcanic sequences such as Cerros del Rincon-Tultul-

Del Medio-Pocitos-El Queva-El Azufre and the Acay systems. Large ignimbrite fields, 

calderas, pyroclastic fields, as well as hot spring systems have contributed to the flowing of 



37 

 

calcium-magnesium and sodium-potassium-lithium and boron enriched solutions that have 

flowed into the basins since the early Tertiary period.  

 

The lithology of the area is comprised of Precambrian meta-sediments consisting of 

slates and phyllites of the Puncoviscana Formation, and Lower Ordovician turbidites (shales 

and sandstone) of the Caucota and Copalayo Formations. These sedimentary units are intruded 

by Late Ordovician granitoids (Oire Eruptive Complex or Eruptive Belt of the Puna), that 

consist of feldspar dacitic porphyries, granites and granodiorites. These rocks are overlain by 

Tertiary continental sedimentary units (Pastos Grandes Group/Geste, Pozuelos, Sijes, Singuel 

Formations) that consist of red-beds, tuffs, halites, borates and gypsum.  The Tertiary 

sedimentary units are in places covered by Upper Miocene volcanics which include dacitic 

lava flows and subvolcanic intrusions (Aguas Calientes Formation), dacitic tuffs, and 

ignimbrites of the Tajamar formation. The sequence is covered in places by Recent-Quaternary 

sediments (Turner, 1972). 

 

7.2  LOCAL GEOLOGY  
 

The geology of the Pastos Grandes area includes Precambrian meta-sedimentary slates 

and phyllite rocks of the Puncoviscana Formation, and Lower Ordovician turbidites, shales 

and sandstone of the Caucota and Copalayo Formations.  These are intruded by Late 

Ordovician granitoids (Complejo Eruptive Oire and the Faja Eruptiva de la Puna, dacitic 

porphyries, granites and granodiorites) and a Tertiary continental sedimentary cover (Pastos 

Grandes Group/Geste, Pozuelos, Sijes, Singuel Formations). The Tertiary rocks include red-

beds, tuffs, halite, borates, gypsum, upper Miocene volcanics built up of dacitic lava flows and 

subvolcanic intrusions (Aguas Calientes Formation), Miocene dacitic tuffs and ignimbrites of 

the Tajamar Formation, and Quaternary sediments (Figures 7.2 and 7.3) (Jordan and Alonso, 

1987). 

 



38 

 

 

Figure 7.2.  Stratigraphic Column for Salar de Pastos Grandes District  
Lopez (2016) 

 

7.3  GEOLOGY OF SALAR DE PASTOS GRANDES 
 

The Pastos Grandes Salar is the current expression of a larger sedimentary basin, 

known as the Sijes Basin, developed and deposited since the Miocene period (7 - 5 million 

years (mA)). The Sijes Formation is represented by sandstone, silt, clay, tuff and evaporite 

such as halite, gypsum, borate and travertine. Its sedimentary thickness is over 400 meters.  

The Singuel Formation (4 - 3 mA) is composed of clastic and volcanic material and crops out 

southeast of the Pastos Grandes Salar. Both geological units (Sijes and Singuel Formations) 

are folded and faulted along a north-northeast – south-southwest trending homoclinal structure, 

with the oldest rocks to the west (Figure 7.3). 

 

	



39 

 

	

Figure 7.3.  Geology of Pastos Grandes District  

5: Precambrian (Puncoviscana Formation),  
17: Ordovician (Copalayo Formation),  
26: Late Ordovician granitoids (Complejo Eruptivo Oire),  
39: Oligocene - Early Miocene (Geste Formation),  
40: Early Miocene (Pozuelos Formation),  
44: Late Miocene (Sijes Formation), 
45: Late Miocene (Singuel Formation),  
46 - 47: Upper Miocene (Tajamar Formation, subvolcanics and volcanics),  
48: Upper Miocene (volcanics),  
50: Upper Miocene (Tajamar Formation, tuffs and ignimbrites),  
51: Recent sediments in alluvial fan (gravel, sand),  
52: Recent salar sediments (clay, sand, halite, borates).  

 

Figure 7.4 shows the local geology of the Salar de Pastos Grande basin.  The Blanca 

Lila Formation (age: <2mA) crops out as patches within and outside the present salar outline, 

occupying an area of 17 km north-south by 8.5 km in an east-west direction. This unit consists 

of sub-horizontal terraces of clastic material (sand, silt and clay) and evaporites (halite and 



40 

 

minor borate). Its thickness as derived from surface mapping was estimated at over 50 meters 

(Alonso and Menegatti, 1990; Alonso 1999; Jordan et al., 1999). Recent drilling suggest the 

Blanca Lila Formation is part of a cyclic clastic-evaporite paleo-salar sequence, in places 350 

meters thick or more. 

 

The present-day Pastos Grandes Salar is filled with clastic unconsolidated sediments 

(clay, sand and silt), organic material and fine-grained sediments. Evaporites are represented 

by halite (NaCl), gypsum (CaSO4) and ulexite (B5O9CaNa.8H2O).  The age of these sediments 

is Late Quaternary to Recent and the thickness is unknown. The bedding is horizontal and 

covers the pre-existing salar formations. Geological features suggest erosion - dissolution of 

older rocks and subsidence of the central part of the salar. The sediments host brine, rich in 

lithium, as demonstrated by Eramine during their 2011 - 2012 campaign. 

 



41 

 

 
Figure 7.4.  Geology Map of Salar de Pastos Grandes  

(modified from Blasco et al. 1996) 



42 

 

 

7.4.  MINERALIZATION 
 

Ulexite hosted in Quaternary and modern sediments is common in the eastern side of 

the salar, close to the road to Sijes. It occurs either as small rounded concretions ("papas") in 

red clays and brown sands, or as a continuous layer ("barra"). Ulexite and colemanite are 

observed in the horizons of borates hosted in the Sijes Formation (Late Miocene) outcropping 

east and south of the Pastos Grandes Salar. These minerals have been mined by Borax 

Argentina and Ulex since 1970, and 1984, respectively.  

 

Halite is a common mineral within the Salar Pastos Grandes and is mined by digging 

pools of 4 meter by 1 meter wide and 1 meter deep (Figure 7.5). The pools become filled with 

brine rich in NaCl. The water evaporates at a rate of 6-8 mm/day, concentrating crystalline 

halite. Each pool is filled with salt in 5 to 6 months and "harvested" with backhoes or loaders. 

It is accumulated for dehydration before being shipped in bulk or in large bags. 

 

 
Figure 7.5.  General View of the Salar de Pastos Grandes Showing Salt Ponds 

 



43 

 

Mineralization at the Property includes lithium-potassium rich – relatively low 

magnesium brine as detected through surface sampling and drilling. The brine can be classified 

as a sulphate-chloride type with anomalous lithium. Lithium concentrations at the surface at 

Pastos Grande Salar range from about 100-300 mg/L. Lithium concentrations increase with 

depth, with holes as shallow as 10 meters reporting as much as 400 mg/L.  A value of 578 

mg/L was reported at a depth of 30 meters in borehole DW01PG. The highest values at surface 

occur along the western margin of the salar, while at depth the values increase towards the 

south. Potassium-lithium ratios throughout the drilled area are in the 10:1 to 12:1 range. This 

is comparable to Salar de Hombre Muerto, where process simulations indicated a rate of 

production for potash at slightly less than 4 tonnes of potash for each tonne of LCE produced. 

  

Average magnesium:lithium ratio is about 6.3:1 based on recent exploration drilling 

and testing. The lowest values found in the lithium triangle are in the range of 2.5:1.  

 

  



44 

 

8.  DEPOSIT TYPES 
 
 

The Salar de Pastos Grandes is classified as a "Silver Peak, Nevada" type, terrigenous 

salar.  Silver Peak, Nevada, USA was the first lithium-bearing brine deposit exploited in the 

world. These deposits are characteristic of arid zone, restricted basins within deep structural 

depressions in-filled with inter-bedded clays, salt (halite), sands and gravels. In the Salar de 

Pastos Grandes, a lithium-bearing aquifer developed during arid climatic periods under similar 

conditions.  

 

Continental brines are the primary source of lithium products worldwide. Bradley et al. 

(2013) noted that "all producing lithium brine deposits share a number of first-order 

characteristics: (1) arid climate; (2) closed basin containing a playa or salar; (3) tectonically 

driven subsidence; (4) associated igneous or geothermal activity; (5) suitable lithium source-

rocks; (6) one or more adequate aquifers; and (7) sufficient time to concentrate a brine." The 

Li atom does not readily form evaporite minerals, and thus remains in solution and concentrates 

to high levels. Large deposits are mined in the Salar de Atacama (SQM and Albemarle), Salar 

de Hombre Muerto, Argentina (FMC) and Clayton Valley, Nevada (Albemarle). 

 

Lithium brine deposit models have been discussed by Houston et al. (2011). Houston 

classified the salars in the Altiplano-Puna region of the Central Andes, South America in terms 

of two end members, "immature clastic" or "mature halite", primarily using (1) the relative 

amount of clastic versus evaporite sediment; (2) climatic and tectonic influences, as related to 

altitude and latitude; and (3) basic hydrology, which controls the influx of fresh water. The 

immature classification refers to basins that generally occur at higher (wetter) elevations in the 

north and east of the region, contain alternating clastic and evaporite sedimentary sequences 

dominated by gypsum, have recycled salts, and a general low abundance of halite. Mature 

refers to salars in arid to hyperarid climates, which occur in the lower elevations of the region, 

reach halite saturation, and have intercalated clay and silt and/or volcanic deposits. An 

important point made by Houston et al. (2011) is the relative significance of aquifer 

permeability which is controlled by the geological and geochemical composition of the 

aquifers.  For example, immature salars may contain large volumes of easily extractable Li-



45 

 

rich brines simply because they are comprised of a mixture of clastic and evaporite aquifer 

materials that have higher porosity and permeability.  

 

Recent discoveries, particularly in northern Argentina point out the importance of 

sedimentary sequences in the host basins. Discoveries since 2010 in the Cauchari, Olaroz and 

Centenario salars involved deeper, early basin in-fill coarse sediments hosting lithium- and 

potassium-enriched brines. It appears that as the regional tectonic relaxation gave rise to pull-

apart basins, the first sediment to fill these basins were coarse, higher energy sediments derived 

from the nearby steep terrain. These coarser sediments have more and larger pore spaces, 

increasing the transmissivity of the formation. As the runoff and hydrothermal fluids 

concentrated in the closed basins as a result of evaporation, the brine became saturated and 

precipitated halite, while lithium, boron, potassium and other elements became more 

concentrated. As the trapped fluids became brackish and eventually evolved into brines 

containing greater than 10,000 ppm contained salts, the density increased, typically to slightly 

in excess of 1.2 grams per cubic centimeter (g/cm3). The denser brine tends to separate and 

sink beneath fresh water and less saturated solutions, and even to start migrating outwards 

beneath the encroaching fresh water at the basin margins. Lithium concentrations tend to 

increase in a direct relationship to density, thus it is not surprising to find more consistent and 

higher grades at depth. The deeper, more coarse sediments at the same time tend to make higher 

yielding aquifers. The Pastos Grandes program will focus on defining the already known 

occurrence of brine to the depths drilled in previous programs, and in addition will target the 

deeper sections to both increase the resource and to improve well brine yields, which has the 

potential to reduce operating costs if the project is developed. 

 

8.1  CONCEPTUAL MODEL OF SALAR BASINS 
 

The conceptual model for the Pastos Grandes basin, and for its brine aquifer, is based 

on exploration of similar salar basins in Chile, Argentina, and Bolivia.  Salar basins are 

characterized by closed topography and interior drainage.  The lowest exposed portions of 

these basins may contain salt encrusted salars.  Typically, no significant groundwater 

discharges from these basins as underflow.  All groundwater discharge that occurs within the 



46 

 

basin is via evaporation.  All surface water that flows into the basin is either evaporated 

directly, or enters the groundwater circulation system and is evaporated at a later time. 

 

Salar basin locations and basin depths are typically structurally controlled, but may be 

influenced by volcanism that may alter drainage patterns.  Basin-fill deposits within salar 

basins typically contain thin to thickly bedded evaporite deposits in the deeper, low-energy 

portion of the basin, together with thin to thickly bedded low-permeability lacustrine clays.  

Coarser-grained, higher permeability deposits associated with active alluvial fans can typically 

be observed along the edges of the salar.  Similar alluvial fan deposits, associated with ancient 

drainages, may occur buried within the basin-fill deposits.  Other permeable basin-fill deposits 

which may occur within salar basins include pyroclastic deposits, ignimbrite flows, lava-flow 

rocks, and spring deposits. 

 

 

8.2  CONCEPTUAL MODEL OF PASTOS GRANDES BASIN 
 

Conceptual hydrogeologic sections were prepared incorporating results of the 

exploration drilling program.  Locations of sections are shown in Figure A-1 (Appendix A); 

sections are shown in Figures A-2 and A-3 (Appendix A).  Well construction schematics and 

lithologic summaries for all of the boreholes and wells drilled by Millennial through 

November, 2017 are included in Figures B-1 through B-22 (Appendix B).  

 

In general, the Pastos Grandes basin has an evaporite core that is dominated by halite, 

although frequently interbedded with thin beds of mixed sand, silt, and/or clay, that is underlain 

by mixed sediments dominated by sand and silt of varying consolidation.  Basin margins are 

interpreted to be fault controlled. Tertiary rocks are common along western basin margin. The 

north margin is dominated by alluvial sediments and is also believed to be fault-controlled.  

Millennial has concessions that include the western and northern parts of the basin (Figure 

4.2). 

 



47 

 

Within the basin, and in the south part of the basin, isolated halite and clay outcrops 

(islands) occur and are as much as 20 meters higher than the salt flat (Figure A-1).  Boreholes 

PGMW17-08 and -8b (Figures B-17 and B-18) were drilled on one of these islands (Figure 

A-1).  Based on the stratigraphy underlying the island (Figure A-3) there is sufficient 

correlation with nearby boreholes drilled on the salt flat to assume there is no substantial 

difference in the sediments underlying the "islands" to salt flat areas adjacent to these outcrops.  

 

Tertiary rocks on the west basin margin are assumed to have low hydraulic conductivity 

and may be a "no-flow" groundwater boundary.  This assumption has not been tested, but at 

this point in the exploration program, our assumption is conservative with respect to lithium 

brine possibly being present in the sediments to the west.  Future evaluation may include VES 

surveys and/or exploration drilling to determine if there is brine potential in the western 

concessions. 

 

Alluvial sediments at the north basin boundary have been shown by recent exploration 

drilling to be permeable, and also to bear lithium brine underneath a fresh water aquifer in the 

upper part. Brine was not encountered at well PGMW17-11 until a depth of about 200 meters 

(Figure B-21).  Fresh water enters the salar from the north as perennial surface water flow, 

and stays in the upper part of the aquifer in the alluvial deposits north from the salar due to 

density differences with the brine.  The brine in the northern alluvial sediments is believed to 

have moved north into those permeable sediments via density-driven flow.  The fresh water to 

the north effectively "floats" on the denser brine and flows south toward the salar until it is 

discharged at the basin margin.  Separating the upper fresh water aquifer and the lower brine 

aquifer is believed to be a mixing zone, but depth-specific sampling at well PGMW17-11 did 

not show strong evidence for a thick brackish mixing zone between the fresh water and the 

deeper brine. 

 

Fine-grained deposits are common near the surface of Salar de Pastos Grandes.  These 

deposits are interpreted to have low hydraulic conductivity, but in general are relatively thin 

(Figures A-2 and A-3). In the north part of the basin, this surface may restrict downward flow 

of water that enters the basin principally from the north.  In addition, hydraulic conductivity in 



48 

 

the vertical direction of groundwater flow (Kz) is typically less than hydraulic conductivity in 

the horizontal direction (Kh).  For layered sediments, such as those that occur in Salar de Pastos 

Grandes, the ratio Kz/Kh is commonly assumed to be 0.01 or less (Freeze and Cherry, 1979, 

p. 34). 

 

Near to the surface, below the upper fine-grained sediments, halite is observed.   The 

evaporite core corresponds to halite deposits with sand or clay filling cavities or fractures. Also 

present are thin, interbedded layers of sand, silt, and/or clay. Unit thickness varies from 100 to 

368 meters, increasing in thickness from north to south (Figure A-2), and also from east to 

west (Figure A-3).  The halite is at times massive with poor permeability, but more often is 

interbedded with more permeable units that allowed depth-specific brine samples to be 

obtained. 

 

Below the halite unit, is a clastic unit consisting of mostly finer-grained sediments with 

coarse sand typically being the largest grain size.  Cementation with halite is common. In 

general, it is weakly to moderately lithified, and in some zones, completely unconsolidated.  

The unconsolidated "running" sand was encountered in most core holes and strongly interfered 

with the coring process.  In some drill holes (PGMW17-04, PGMW17-04b, PGMW17-5b, 

PGMW17-06c, PGMW17-08 and PGMW17-09 – Appendix B) this unit was completely 

penetrated and overlies a semi-consolidated, permeable, fine-grained sandstone unit.  

 

Depth to water in the salar is typically within 1 meter of land surface on the salt flat 

(Appendix B). The exception to this is the depth to water at well PGMW17-08 because the 

well is located on one of the remnant "islands" in the basin which is about 17 meters above the 

salt flat (Figure A-1).   Depth to water in the north concessions area outside of the salar at well 

PGMW17-11 was measured to be about 31 meters below land surface (Figure B-21).  

 

8.3  ASSIGNMENT OF HYDROGEOLOGIC UNITS 
 

Results of exploration drilling indicate that basin-fill, lacustrine deposits in Salar Pastos 

Verdes can be divided into hydrogeologic units that are dominated by five lithologies defined 



49 

 

by grain size and stratigraphy.  Except for the gravel/breccia unit, which was not sampled for 

brine, all of the units have been sampled and analyzed for both drainable porosity and brine 

chemistry. Predominant lithologies, meters drilled, and number of analyses are given in Table 

8.1. 

 

Table 8.1.  Sample Data for Hydrogeologic Units

Information from both drill core and cuttings was useful to describe and separate the 

hydrogeologic units. Basin surfaces are mostly covered by the clay unit and with evaporite 

sediments in parts.  Thick clay layers are relatively uncommon in the basin, but thin layers are 

relatively common.  These thin clay and silt beds are included in the mixed halite and mixed 

sand and silt units.  

 

Relatively few brine samples were attempted and/or obtained from the halite unit 

because of the local massive nature of the unit.  However, depth-specific packer sampling did 

produce brine samples from the mixed halite unit, especially when interbedded with clastic 

sediments.  Most brine samples were obtained from the mixed sand and silt unit which 

represents the most abundant unit in the upper 600 meters of the basin.  

  



50 

 

9.  EXPLORATION  
 
 
 Phase I non-drilling exploration activities previously conducted by Eramine are 

documented in this section.  In addition, results of the VES electrical survey conducted by 

Millennial during 2017 in the north part of the concession area as part of the Phase II 

exploration are documented. 

 

9.1  PREVIOUS STUDIES 
 

Interpretation of past works has focused mainly on the non-drilling exploration 

activities reported by Eramine. The Phase I exploration work completed at Salar de Pastos 

Grandes by Eramine covered a larger area that includes the first Millennial Properties. The 

objective of Eramine's program was to evaluate the potential for lithium of the salar and 

consisted of the following: 

▪ Surface Brine Sampling: Brine samples from shallow pits were collected 
throughout the salar to obtain preliminary indications of lithium occurrence and 

distribution. 

▪ Transient Electromagnetic (TEM) Survey: A TEM survey was completed to 
attempt definition of fresh water/brine interfaces around the salar perimeter.  

▪ Vertical Electrical Sounding (VES) Survey: A VES survey was conducted to 
define the brine and fresh water interface. 

9.1.1  Surface Water and Brines 
 

In 2011, a total of nine surface brine samples were collected from shallow hand-dug 

auger test pits excavated throughout the project area (Figure 9.1). Results from this early 

program indicated favorable potential for significant lithium grades at depth. Additional 

exploration work was then planned on the basis of these results. 

 



51 

 

 

Figure 9.1.  Surface Brine Sample Distribution and Chemistry Results in Salar 
de Pastos Grandes 

 



52 

 

Three brine samples collected in the southwestern sector of the properties under study returned 

values between 602.2 to 665.9 mg/L of lithium and 6,342 to 7,146 mg/L of potassium. Two 

samples in the south-central area recorded values of 268.3 and 288.2 mg/L lithium. Samples 

taken in the northern sectors, collected outside the salt crust, assayed lithium values of 0.2 to 

9.7 mg/L and potassium values of 7 to 369 mg/L. The average Mg/Li ratio from the Phase I 

campaign is 8:1; K/Li ratio is 11:1, calculated for the five samples shown in red in Table 9.1.

9.1.2  TEM Survey 
	

A TEM survey was conducted by Eramine in the Salar de Pastos Grandes, utilizing six 

TEM points. The main objective of the survey was to test the ability of the method to determine 

resistivity contrasts which may relate to changes in groundwater salinity.  In general, saline 

brines are more conductive (lower resistivity), whereas areas of fresh water are less conductive 

(higher resistivity). The details, results and conclusions of the survey were not published or 

otherwise disclosed by Eramine and are thus not included in this report. 



53 

 

9.1.3  VES Survey 
 

A VES survey was conducted by Eramine in the salar. The objectives of the program 

were to: 1) Determine the distribution of brine and fresh water at depth; and 2) compare VES 

survey results with TEM survey. The VES survey points were: PG03VES, PG04VES, 

CentroVES, EsteVES, and PG05VES. The available VEG survey point locations (VEG_PG03 

and VEG_PG04) are shown in Figure 9.2. 

 

VES_PG03 shows a resistivity of 0.4 ohms/meter (ohm/m) between 2 and 72 meters 

below land surface (brine); another area of 0.28 ohm/m occurs between 120 and 210 meters 

below land surface (brine) and below a depth of 210 meters, a body of halite brine was 

interpreted (10 ohm/m, Figure 9.3).   

 

 

Figure 9.2.  VES Survey Points, Salar de Pastos Grandes 



54 

 

 

Figure 9.3.  Interpretation of VESPG03 - Vertical Section 
 

VES_PG04 shows resistivity of 0.31 ohm/m between 19 and 51 meters below land 

surface (brine); a halite body with brine was interpreted to occur below 51 meters (2.3 ohm/m, 

Figure 9.4). 

 

Figure 9.4.  Interpretation of VES_PG04 - Vertical Section 
 

VES_Centro (no section or location available) reports a resistivity of 0.28 ohm/m 

between 13 and 24 meters below land surface (brine); halite was interpreted to occur between 



55 

 

24 and 180 meters based on 70 ohm/m; and below 180 meters, brine with a resistivity of 0.2 

ohm/m was interpreted.  

 

VES_Este (no section or location available) reported a resistivity of 0.19 ohm/m 

between 6.5 and 16 meter below land surface (brine); and halite was interpreted to be present 

below 16 meters based on resistivity value of 60 ohm/meter. 

 

VES_PG05 (no section or location available) reported a resistivity of 0.7 ohm/km 

between land surface and 9 meters (brine); between 9 and 19m, resistivity ass 0.2 ohms/m 

(brine); and below 19 meters, halite with resistivity of 104 ohm/meter was interpreted. 

 

In summary, the results of the VES survey appear to show relatively shallow, thick 

bodies of halite. However, drilling in the same areas also penetrated interbedded layers of salt. 

Massive salt typically does not imply good permeability.  

 

9.1.4  Controlled Source Audio-Frequency Magnetotelluric (CSAMT) Survey 
 

A CSAMT survey was conducted at Salar de Pastos Grandes by Eramine/Bolera 

Minera S.A. in 2011.  The objectives of this survey were to: 1) Determine the distribution of 

conductive bodies (salt water or clay) within the salar; and 2) compare CSAMT survey results 

with TEM and VES (Figure 9.5). The CSAMT survey points are: PP1, PP2, P4, P6, P7, P8, 

P12, P13/19, P18 and P29. CSAMT stations were used to draw 2-dimensional (2D) vertical 

sections. 



56 

 

 

Figure 9.5.  CSAMT and 2D Lines in Salar de Pastos Grandes 

 

Line 5 of the CSAMT survey consists of a 2D section with east-west orientation located 

in the central-south part of the property. It shows a resistivity <1 ohm/m from 0-70 meters, 

which suggests a conductive body comparable with a brine aquifer. Below 110 meters, 

resistivity of >5ohm/m suggests a more massive saline body (Figure 9.6). 



57 

 

 
Figure 9.6.  CSAMT Line 5, 2D Vertical Section 

 

Line 6 of the CSAMT survey corresponds to a 2D section in west-northwest – east-

southeast orientation. The section is in the central-south sector of the properties and shows a 

resistivity of <1 ohm/m, intercalated with other horizontal sectors showing resistivity between 

1 to 2.5ohm/m to a depth of 120 meters below land surface. No CSAMT measurements below 

120 meters are recorded (Figure 9.7). 



58 

 

 
Figure 9.7.  CSAMT Line 6, 2D Vertical Section 

 

Line 8 of the CSAMT survey is a 2D section with a west-east orientation, and is located 

in the central part of the property.  The measurements suggest lenticular bodies with resistivity 

<1 ohm/m, being intercalated with other minor bodies with a resistivity between 1 to 3 ohm/m, 

to a depth of 290 meters below land surface (Figure 9.8). The CSAMT survey in general 

reflects the same relationships as VES, but for larger depths.  

 
Figure 9.8.  CSAMT Line 8, 2D Vertical Section 



59 

 

9.2  2017 VES SURVEY  
 

As part of the Phase II exploration program, a second VES survey was conducted by 

Millennial in the northern concession area in the alluvial fan material north of the salar.  

Locations for the geophysical points/lines at the north edge of the salar are shown in Figure 

9.9.  It had been assumed that the salar brine had moved north into the clastic sediments north 

of the salar via density movement.  It is clear from the surface that fresh water enters the salar 

from the north, but it was surmised that brine may still be present below the fresh water in the 

concession area.  Therefore, to test this theory, a VES geophysical survey program was 

designed and implemented with the objectives to: 1) Determine the distribution of brine and 

fresh water at depth; 2) Extend the potential Inferred resource to the north; and 3) Identify an 

exploration drilling location and support specifications for the well.  

 
Figure 9.9.  VES Points and Lines, North Part of Pastos Grandes 



60 

 

 

Figures 9.10, 9.11, and 9.12 show the interpreted profiles of the South to North line, 

and two West to East lines. For reference, monitor well PGMW17-11 is located approximately 

half-way between SEV-02 and SEV-09 on the North-South profile (Figures 9.9 and 9.10).  

Monitoring well PGMW17-11 was drilled because the results of the VES survey suggested 

subsurface brine occurred in this concession area. 

 

Although the exact boundaries of the unsaturated zone (dark area near surface in Figure 

9.10), freshwater zone (light blue), brine (orange), and highly conductive brine (dashes) are 

not perfectly correct regarding specific depths to these boundaries, conceptually they are 

considered acceptable.  During drilling and sampling at well PGMW17-11, lithium-rich brine 

was encountered in a sedimentary aquifer below a depth of about 200 meters approximately 

where the highly-conductive brine interface was interpreted (Figure 9.10). 

 

Figure 9.10.  Interpretation of South to North Vertical Section 



61 

 

 
Figure 9.11.  Interpretation of Southernmost West to East Vertical Section

Figure 9.12.  Interpretation of Northernmost West to East Vertical Section

10.  DRILLING & PUMPING TESTS 
 
 

Drilling and testing work to date has included previous investigations during the Phase 

I exploration program, and recently-completed Phase II exploration field operations conducted 

by Millennial.  Results of the Phase II investigations presented in this report are from field 

work completed as of November, 2017.  Phase III exploration field work is ongoing as of the 

date of this report; results from Phase III work will be documented in a future report.  

 

10.1  PREVIOUS STUDIES FROM ERAMINE PHASE I EXPLORATION 
 

Phase I drilling and testing activities have been conducted by Eramine and included the 

drilling of 11 shallow exploration wells ("SW" wells), two diamond drill holes (DW01PGDDH 

and DW02PGDDH), four shallow exploration holes constructed with 6-inch casing ("PMP" 

wells), and three exploration wells of varying depths constructed with 6-inch casing 

(DW03PG, DW04PG, DW05PG).  Detailed information for the wells has not been published 

and mostly is unavailable. 

 

Constant-rate pumping tests were conducted at wells DW03PG, SW04cPG, and 

DW05PG.  A summary of test logistics is given in Table 10.1. Locations for the tested wells 

are shown on Figure 10.1.

10.1.3  Well SW04cPG 
 

Well SW04cPG was located in the central portion of the Salar de Pastos Grandes, at 

S24.56960595°, W66.68864091°, Datum WGS84 and collar elevation 3,785 masl at the 

surface of the Salar. The well was drilled to a depth of 20 meters and constructed with 6-inch 

casing. 

 

During testing at well SW04cPG, wells DW04PG, SW04bPG and SW04PG were used 

as monitor wells.  No information is available regarding the distance between the monitor wells 

and the pumping well.  The well was pumped at a constant rate of 5.67 L/s and water level 

drawdown at the monitor wells were as follows: 

▪ DW04PG: average drawdown of 0.76 meters 
▪ SW04bPG: average drawdown of 0.92 meters 
▪ SW04PG: average drawdown of 0.81 meters 

 

During the 96 hours of pumping, the density of the brine was relatively constant at 1.22 

gm/cm3.
